	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Oriental Orthodox Churches</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodoxy&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Oriental Orthodoxy">Oriental Orthodoxy</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="image"><img alt="P christianity.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/16px-P_christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="14" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/24px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/32px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Oriental_Orthodox_sidebar" title="Template:Oriental Orthodox sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Oriental_Orthodox_sidebar" title="Template talk:Oriental Orthodox sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Oriental_Orthodox_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table> 
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><td style="background:lavender;;padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Christianity" title="Category:Christianity">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th style="background:lavender;;padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em"><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em"><a href="/wiki/File:Christian_cross.svg" class="image" title="Principal symbol of Christianity"><img alt="Principal symbol of Christianity" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Christian_cross.svg/75px-Christian_cross.svg.png" decoding="async" width="75" height="105" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Christian_cross.svg/113px-Christian_cross.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Christian_cross.svg/150px-Christian_cross.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="404" data-file-height="564" /></a></td></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">

<a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="image" title="Christian cross"><img alt="Christian cross" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/20px-P_christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/30px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/40px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christianity_sidebar" title="Template talk:Christianity sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christianity_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Oriental Orthodox Churches</b> are a group of <a href="/wiki/Christian_churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian churches">Christian churches</a> adhering to <a href="/wiki/Miaphysitism" title="Miaphysitism">miaphysite</a> <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a> and theology, and together have 60 to 70 million members worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> As some of the oldest religious institutions in the world, the Oriental Orthodox Churches have played a prominent role in the history and culture of <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nubia#Christian_Nubia" title="Nubia">Sudan</a> and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. An <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christian</a> body of <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">churches</a>, its <a href="/wiki/Bishop_(Eastern_Orthodox_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop (Eastern Orthodox Church)">bishops</a> are equal by virtue of <a href="/wiki/Consecration#Ordination_of_bishops" title="Consecration">episcopal ordination</a>, and its doctrines can be summarized in that the churches recognize the validity of only the first three <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">ecumenical councils</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Oriental Orthodox Churches are composed of six autocephalous churches: the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church_of_Antioch" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac Orthodox Church of Antioch">Syriac Orthodox Church of Antioch</a> (including its archdiocese in India the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_Syrian_Christian_Church" title="Jacobite Syrian Christian Church">Jacobite Syrian Christian Church</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eritrean_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Eritrean Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Eritrean Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Malankara_Orthodox_Syrian_Church" title="Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church">Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Collectively, they consider themselves to be the <a href="/wiki/Four_Marks_of_the_Church" title="Four Marks of the Church">one, holy, catholic, and apostolic church</a> founded by <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">Great Commission</a>, and that its <a href="/wiki/Bishop_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop (Catholic Church)">bishops</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_succession" title="Apostolic succession">successors</a> of Christ's <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">apostles</a>. Most member churches are part of the <a href="/wiki/World_Council_of_Churches" title="World Council of Churches">World Council of Churches</a>. Three very different rites are practiced among the churches: the western-influenced <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Rite" title="Armenian Rite">Armenian Rite</a>, the <a href="/wiki/West_Syrian_Rite" class="mw-redirect" title="West Syrian Rite">West Syrian Rite</a> of the two Syriac churches, and the <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_Rite" title="Alexandrian Rite">Alexandrian Rite</a> of the Copts, Ethiopians and Eritreans.
</p><p>Oriental Orthodox Churches shared <a href="/wiki/Communion_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Communion (Christian)">communion</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> before the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Chalcedon" title="Council of Chalcedon">Council of Chalcedon</a> in AD 451, as well as with the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a> in AD 431, all separating primarily over <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Definition" title="Chalcedonian Definition">differences in</a> <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>.
</p><p>The Oriental Orthodox developed distinctively under the Patriarchate of Alexandria in Egypt, originally part of the <a href="/wiki/Pentarchy" title="Pentarchy">Pentarchy</a>, and the only episcopal see besides the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">See of Rome</a> to maintain the title "<a href="/wiki/List_of_Coptic_Orthodox_Popes_of_Alexandria" title="List of Coptic Orthodox Popes of Alexandria">Pope</a>". The majority of Oriental Orthodox Christians live in Egypt, Ethiopia, Eritrea, and Armenia, with smaller <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Syriac</a> communities living in the Middle East&#8212;decreasing due to <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians" title="Persecution of Christians">persecution</a>&#8212;and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. There are also many in other parts of the world, formed through <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">conversions</a>, and missionary activity.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_characteristics">Name and characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name and characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name "Oriental Orthodox Churches" was coined for the <a href="/wiki/Conference_of_Addis_Ababa" title="Conference of Addis Ababa">Conference of Addis Ababa</a> in 1965. At the time there were five participating churches, the Eritrean church not yet being autocephalous.<sup id="cite_ref-Boutros_Ghali2_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boutros_Ghali2-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other names by which the churches have been known include Old Oriental, Ancient Oriental, Lesser Eastern, Anti-Chalcedonian, Non-Chalcedonian, Pre-Chalcedonian, Miaphysite or Monophysite,<sup id="cite_ref-Keshishian2_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keshishian2-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> although the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> is equally anti-, non- and pre-Chalcedonian.<sup id="cite_ref-Boutros_Ghali2_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boutros_Ghali2-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Oriental Orthodox Churches are distinguished by their recognition of only the first three ecumenical councils during the period of the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a>&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> in 325, the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Constantinople" title="First Council of Constantinople">First Council of Constantinople</a> in 381 and the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a> in 431. Oriental Orthodoxy shares much theology and many <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiology" title="Ecclesiology">ecclesiastical</a> traditions with the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>; these include a similar <a href="/wiki/Theosis_(Eastern_Christian_theology)" title="Theosis (Eastern Christian theology)">doctrine of salvation</a><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and a tradition of collegiality between bishops, as well as reverence of the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a> and use of the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary theological difference between the two communions is the differing Christology. Oriental Orthodoxy rejects the <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Definition" title="Chalcedonian Definition">Chalcedonian Definition</a>, and instead adopts the <a href="/wiki/Miaphysite" class="mw-redirect" title="Miaphysite">Miaphysite</a> formula, believing that the human and <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a> natures of Christ are united. Historically, the early prelates of the Oriental Orthodox Churches thought that the Chalcedonian Definition implied a possible repudiation of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> or a concession to <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a>.
</p><p>Other differences include minor deviations in social teaching and different views on <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">ecumenism</a>. The Oriental Orthodox churches are generally considered to be more conservative with regard to social issues as well more enthusiastic about ecumenical relations with non-Orthodox churches.
</p><p>The break in communion between the Imperial Roman and Oriental Orthodox churches did not occur suddenly, but rather gradually over 2-3 centuries following the Council of Chalcedon.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Eventually the two communions developed separate institutions, and the Oriental Orthodox did not participate in any of the later ecumenical councils.
</p><p>The Oriental Orthodox Churches maintain their own ancient apostolic succession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKrikorian201045,_128,_181,_194,_206_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKrikorian201045,_128,_181,_194,_206-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The various churches are governed by <a href="/wiki/Holy_Synod" title="Holy Synod">holy synods</a>, with a <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus inter pares</a></i> bishop serving as <a href="/wiki/Primate_(bishop)" title="Primate (bishop)">primate</a>. The primates hold titles like <a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholicos" title="Catholicos">catholicos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pope_of_the_Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Pope of the Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">pope</a>. Among these patriarchs, the <a href="/wiki/Pope_of_the_Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Pope of the Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Pope of Alexandria</a> takes precedence, and is sometimes considered the "face" of Oriental Orthodoxy. The Alexandrian Patriarchate, along with <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Antioch" title="Patriarch of Antioch">Antioch</a>, was one of the most prominent sees of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">the early Christian Church</a>, and contains a majority population of Coptic Christians, and unlike Antioch is still a major population center.
</p><p>That said, the Pope of Alexandria has no governing powers with respect to the non-Coptic churches. Oriental Orthodoxy does not have a magisterial leader like the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, nor does the communion have a leader who can convene ecumenical synods like the Eastern Orthodox Church.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Chalcedonian_Christology">Non-Chalcedonian Christology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Non-Chalcedonian Christology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Oriental_Orthodoxy#Chalcedonian_Schism" title="History of Oriental Orthodoxy">History of Oriental Orthodoxy §&#160;Chalcedonian Schism</a></div>
<p>The schism between Oriental Orthodoxy and the adherents of Chalcedonian Christianity was based on differences in Christology. The First Council of Nicaea, in 325, declared that Jesus Christ is <a href="/wiki/God_in_Christianity" title="God in Christianity">God</a>, that is to say, "<a href="/wiki/Consubstantial" class="mw-redirect" title="Consubstantial">consubstantial</a>" with the Father. Later, the third ecumenical council, the Council of Ephesus, declared that Jesus Christ, though divine as well as human, is only one being, or person (<a href="/wiki/Hypostatic_union" title="Hypostatic union">hypostasis</a>). Thus, the Council of Ephesus explicitly rejected <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a>, the Christological doctrine that Christ was two distinct beings, one divine (the Logos) and one human (Jesus), who happened to inhabit the same body. The churches that later became Oriental Orthodoxy were firmly anti-Nestorian, and therefore strongly supported the decisions made at Ephesus.
</p><p>Twenty years after Ephesus, the Council of Chalcedon reaffirmed the view that Jesus Christ was a single person, but at the same time declared that this one person existed "in two complete natures", one human and one divine. Those who opposed Chalcedon saw this as a concession to Nestorianism, or even as a conspiracy to convert the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Christian Church</a> to Nestorianism by stealth. As a result, over the following decades, they gradually separated from communion with those who accepted the Council of Chalcedon, and formed the body that is today called the Oriental Orthodox Churches.
</p><p>At times, Chalcedonian Christians have referred to the Oriental Orthodox as being <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">Monophysites</a>—that is to say, accusing them of following the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Eutyches" title="Eutyches">Eutyches</a> (c. 380 – c. 456), who argued that Jesus Christ was not human at all, but only divine. Monophysitism was condemned as heretical alongside Nestorianism, and to accuse a church of being Monophysite is to accuse it of falling into the opposite extreme from Nestorianism. However, the Oriental Orthodox themselves reject this description as inaccurate, having officially condemned the teachings of both <a href="/wiki/Nestorius" title="Nestorius">Nestorius</a> and Eutyches. They define themselves as Miaphysite instead, holding that Christ has one nature, but this nature is both human and divine.<sup id="cite_ref-first_seven2_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-first_seven2-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the Oriental Orthodox Churches are in <a href="/wiki/Full_communion" title="Full communion">full communion</a> with each other, but not with the Eastern Orthodox Church or any other churches; the Oriental Orthodox Churches while in communion do not form a single church as the Catholics or Eastern Orthodox. Slow dialogue towards restoring communion between the two Orthodox groups began in the mid-20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-sor.cua.edu2_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sor.cua.edu2-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> and dialogue is also underway between Oriental Orthodoxy and the Catholic Church and others.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, the mutual recognition of <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> was restored between the Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria and the Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Also baptism is mutually recognized between the Armenian Apostolic Church and the Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Oriental_Orthodoxy" title="History of Oriental Orthodoxy">History of Oriental Orthodoxy</a></div>
<h3><span id="Post_Council_of_Chalcedon_.28451_AD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_Council_of_Chalcedon_(451_AD)">Post Council of Chalcedon (451 AD)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Post Council of Chalcedon (451 AD)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Schism" title="Schism">schism</a> between the Oriental Orthodox and the rest of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> occurred in the 5th century. The separation resulted in part from the refusal of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Dioscorus_I_of_Alexandria" title="Pope Dioscorus I of Alexandria">Pope Dioscorus I of Alexandria</a> and the other thirteen Egyptian bishops to accept the Christological dogmas promulgated by the Council of Chalcedon, which held that Jesus is in two natures: one divine and one human. They would accept only "of or from two natures" but not "in two natures".
</p><p>To the hierarchs who would lead the Oriental Orthodox, the latter phrase was tantamount to accepting Nestorianism, which expressed itself in a terminology incompatible with their understanding of Christology. Nestorianism was understood as seeing Christ in two separate natures, human and divine, each with different actions and experiences; in contrast <a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Alexandria" title="Cyril of Alexandria">Cyril of Alexandria</a> advocated the formula "One Nature of God the Incarnate Logos"<sup id="cite_ref-Shenouda1999_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shenouda1999-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> (or as others translate,<sup id="cite_ref-CyrilTransPusey_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CyrilTransPusey-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> "One Incarnate Nature of the Word"), stressing the unity of the incarnation over all other considerations. It is not entirely clear that Nestorius himself was a Nestorian.
</p><p>The Oriental Orthodox churches were therefore often called "Monophysite", although they reject this label, as it is associated with <a href="/wiki/Eutychianism" title="Eutychianism">Eutychian Monophysitism</a>; they prefer the term "Miaphysite". Oriental Orthodox churches reject what they consider to be the heretical Monophysite teachings of <a href="/wiki/Apollinaris_of_Laodicea" title="Apollinaris of Laodicea">Apollinaris of Laodicea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eutyches" title="Eutyches">Eutyches</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dyophysite" class="mw-redirect" title="Dyophysite">Dyophysite</a> definition of the Council of Chalcedon and the Antiochene christology of <a href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Mopsuestia" title="Theodore of Mopsuestia">Theodore of Mopsuestia</a>, Nestorius, <a href="/wiki/Theodoret" title="Theodoret">Theodoret</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ibas_of_Edessa" title="Ibas of Edessa">Ibas of Edessa</a>.
</p><p>Christology, although important, was not the only reason for the Alexandrian Church's refusal to accept the declarations of the Council of Chalcedon; political, ecclesiastical and imperial issues were hotly debated during that period.
</p><p>In the years following Chalcedon the patriarchs of Constantinople intermittently remained in communion with the non-Chalcedonian <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Alexandria" title="Patriarch of Alexandria">Patriarchs of Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Antioch" title="Patriarch of Antioch">Antioch</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Henotikon" title="Henotikon">Henotikon</a>), while Rome remained out of communion with the latter and in unstable communion with Constantinople. It was not until 518 that the new Byzantine Emperor, <a href="/wiki/Justin_I" title="Justin I">Justin I</a> (who accepted Chalcedon), demanded that the Church in the Roman Empire accept the Council's decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Justin ordered the replacement of all non-Chalcedonian bishops, including the patriarchs of Antioch and Alexandria. The extent of the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Rome" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Rome">Bishop of Rome</a> in this demand has been a matter of debate. <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> also attempted to bring those monks who still rejected the decision of the Council of Chalcedon into communion with the greater church. The exact time of this event is unknown, but it is believed to have been between 535 and 548.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abraham_(Copt)" class="mw-redirect" title="Abraham (Copt)">St Abraham of Farshut</a> was summoned to Constantinople and he chose to bring with him four monks. Upon arrival, Justinian summoned them and informed them that they would either accept the decision of the Council or lose their positions. Abraham refused to entertain the idea. <a href="/wiki/Theodora_(6th_century)" title="Theodora (6th century)">Theodora</a> tried to persuade Justinian to change his mind, seemingly to no avail.  Abraham himself stated in a letter to his monks that he preferred to remain in exile rather than subscribe to a faith which he believed to be contrary to that of <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_of_Alexandria" title="Athanasius of Alexandria">Athanasius of Alexandria</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the 20th century the Chalcedonian schism was not seen with the same importance, and from several meetings between the authorities of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a> and the Oriental Orthodoxy, reconciling declarations emerged in the common statement of Syriac Patriarch Mar <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Zakka_I_Iwas" title="Ignatius Zakka I Iwas">Ignatius Zakka I Iwas</a> and the Roman <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> in 1984:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The confusions and schisms that occurred between their Churches in the later centuries, they realize today, in no way affect or touch the substance of their faith, since these arose only because of differences in terminology and culture and in the various formulae adopted by different theological schools to express the same matter. Accordingly, we find today no real basis for the sad divisions and schisms that subsequently arose between us concerning the doctrine of Incarnation. In words and life we confess the true doctrine concerning Christ our Lord, notwithstanding the differences in interpretation of such a doctrine which arose at the time of the Council of Chalcedon.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>According to the canons of the Oriental Orthodox churches, the four bishops of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> were all given status as <a href="/wiki/Pentarchy" title="Pentarchy">patriarchs</a>; in other words, the ancient apostolic centres of Christianity, by the First Council of Nicaea (predating the schism)&#8212;each of the four patriarchs was responsible for those bishops and churches within his own area of the Church. Thus, the Bishop of Rome has always been held by the others to be fully sovereign within his own area, as well as "first-among-equals", due to the traditional belief that the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Apostles" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelve Apostles">Apostles</a> <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Saint Peter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Saint Paul</a> were martyred in Rome.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Reliable, non Roman Catholic, source needed for the whole sentence (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The technical reason for the schism was that the bishops of Rome and Constantinople excommunicated the non-Chalcedonian bishops in 451 for refusing to accept the "in two natures" teaching, thus declaring them to be out of communion.
</p><p>The highest office in Oriental Orthodoxy is that of patriarch. There are patriarchs within the local Oriental Orthodox communities of the Coptic, Armenian, Eritrean, Ethiopian, Syriac, and Indian (Malankara) Orthodox churches. The title of pope, as used by the leading bishop of the Coptic Church, has the meaning of "Father" and is not a jurisdictional title.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographical_distribution">Geographical distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geographical distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country" title="Oriental Orthodoxy by country">Oriental Orthodoxy by country</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country.png/400px-Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country.png/600px-Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country.png/800px-Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="882" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oriental_Orthodoxy_by_country.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Distribution of Oriental Orthodox Christians in the world by country:</b> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #550000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Main religion (more than 75%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d40000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Main religion (50–75%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Important minority religion (20–50%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff5555; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Important minority religion (5–20%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffaaaa; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Minority religion (1–5%)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffc8aa; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Tiny minority religion (below 1%), but has local <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephaly</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>According to the Encyclopedia of Religion, Oriental Orthodoxy is the Christian tradition "most important in terms of the number of faithful living in the Middle East", which, along with other <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity#Families_of_churches" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christian communions</a>, represent an <a href="/wiki/Autochthonous_population" class="mw-redirect" title="Autochthonous population">autochthonous</a> Christian presence whose origins date further back than the birth and spread of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>It is the dominant religion in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> (94%) and ethnically <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenian</a> unrecognized <a href="/wiki/Nagorno-Karabakh_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Nagorno-Karabakh Republic">Nagorno-Karabakh Republic</a> (95%).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Oriental Orthodoxy is a prevailing religion in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> (43.1%), while Protestants account for 19.4% and Islam - 34.1%.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> It is most widespread in two regions in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>: <a href="/wiki/Amhara_Region" title="Amhara Region">Amhara</a> (82%) and <a href="/wiki/Tigray_Region" title="Tigray Region">Tigray</a> (96%), as well as the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a> (75%). It is also one of two major religions in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> (40%).<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is a minority in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (&lt;20%),<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> (3–5%),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> (2–3% out of the 10% of total Christians), <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> (10% of the 40% of Christians in Lebanon or 200,000 Armenians and members of the Church of the East) and <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (7% out of the 20% of total Christians in Kerala).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In terms of total number of members, the Ethiopian Church is the largest of all Oriental Orthodox churches, and is second among all Orthodox churches among Eastern and OrientalcChurches (exceeded in number only by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a>).
</p><p>Also of particular importance are the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Armenian Patriarchate of Constantinople">Armenian Patriarchate of Constantinople</a> in Turkey and the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Iran" title="Christianity in Iran">Armenian Apostolic Church of Iran</a>. These Oriental Orthodox churches represent the largest Christian minority in both of these predominantly Muslim countries, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D4%B7%D5%BB%D5%B4%D5%AB%D5%A1%D5%AE%D5%B6%D5%AB_%D5%84%D5%A1%D5%B5%D6%80_%D5%8F%D5%A1%D5%B3%D5%A1%D6%80.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/%D4%B7%D5%BB%D5%B4%D5%AB%D5%A1%D5%AE%D5%B6%D5%AB_%D5%84%D5%A1%D5%B5%D6%80_%D5%8F%D5%A1%D5%B3%D5%A1%D6%80.jpg/200px-%D4%B7%D5%BB%D5%B4%D5%AB%D5%A1%D5%AE%D5%B6%D5%AB_%D5%84%D5%A1%D5%B5%D6%80_%D5%8F%D5%A1%D5%B3%D5%A1%D6%80.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/%D4%B7%D5%BB%D5%B4%D5%AB%D5%A1%D5%AE%D5%B6%D5%AB_%D5%84%D5%A1%D5%B5%D6%80_%D5%8F%D5%A1%D5%B3%D5%A1%D6%80.jpg/300px-%D4%B7%D5%BB%D5%B4%D5%AB%D5%A1%D5%AE%D5%B6%D5%AB_%D5%84%D5%A1%D5%B5%D6%80_%D5%8F%D5%A1%D5%B3%D5%A1%D6%80.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/%D4%B7%D5%BB%D5%B4%D5%AB%D5%A1%D5%AE%D5%B6%D5%AB_%D5%84%D5%A1%D5%B5%D6%80_%D5%8F%D5%A1%D5%B3%D5%A1%D6%80.jpg/400px-%D4%B7%D5%BB%D5%B4%D5%AB%D5%A1%D5%AE%D5%B6%D5%AB_%D5%84%D5%A1%D5%B5%D6%80_%D5%8F%D5%A1%D5%B3%D5%A1%D6%80.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2893" data-file-height="1961" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D4%B7%D5%BB%D5%B4%D5%AB%D5%A1%D5%AE%D5%B6%D5%AB_%D5%84%D5%A1%D5%B5%D6%80_%D5%8F%D5%A1%D5%B3%D5%A1%D6%80.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Etchmiadzin_Cathedral" title="Etchmiadzin Cathedral">Etchmiadzin Armenian Apostolic Cathedral</a> in <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Assouan_cathedrale_copte.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Assouan_cathedrale_copte.jpg/200px-Assouan_cathedrale_copte.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Assouan_cathedrale_copte.jpg/300px-Assouan_cathedrale_copte.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Assouan_cathedrale_copte.jpg/400px-Assouan_cathedrale_copte.jpg 2x" data-file-width="695" data-file-height="596" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Assouan_cathedrale_copte.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a> Coptic Orthodox Cathedral in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coptic_Orthodox_pope,_Syriac_Orthodox_patriarch,_and_Armenian_Apostolic_catholicos.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Coptic_Orthodox_pope%2C_Syriac_Orthodox_patriarch%2C_and_Armenian_Apostolic_catholicos.jpg/220px-Coptic_Orthodox_pope%2C_Syriac_Orthodox_patriarch%2C_and_Armenian_Apostolic_catholicos.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Coptic_Orthodox_pope%2C_Syriac_Orthodox_patriarch%2C_and_Armenian_Apostolic_catholicos.jpg/330px-Coptic_Orthodox_pope%2C_Syriac_Orthodox_patriarch%2C_and_Armenian_Apostolic_catholicos.jpg 1.5x, 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="639" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coptic_Orthodox_pope,_Syriac_Orthodox_patriarch,_and_Armenian_Apostolic_catholicos.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pope <a href="/wiki/Tawadros_II_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawadros II of Alexandria">Tawadros II of Alexandria</a> (centre) of the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Coptic Orthodox Church">Coptic Orthodox Church</a> receives <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_Aphrem_II" title="Ignatius Aphrem II">Ignatius Aphrem II</a> (left) Patriarch of Antioch of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aram_I" title="Aram I">Aram I</a> (right) Supreme Catholicose of the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Orthodox_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Armenian Apostolic Orthodox Church">Armenian Apostolic Orthodox Church</a> in Lebanon.</div></div></div>
<p>The Oriental Orthodox Church is a <a href="/wiki/Full_communion" title="Full communion">communion</a> of 6 <a href="/wiki/Autocephaly" title="Autocephaly">autocephalous</a> (that is, administratively completely independent) regional churches.<sup id="cite_ref-Keshishian2_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keshishian2-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Each church has defined geographical boundaries of its jurisdiction and is ruled by its council of bishops or synod presided by a senior bishop–its primate (or first hierarch). The primate may carry the honorary title of pope (in the Alexandria tradition), patriarch, abuna (in the Ethiopian tradition) or catholicos.
</p><p>Each regional church consists of constituent eparchies (or, dioceses) ruled by a bishop. Some churches have given an eparchy or group of eparchies varying degrees of autonomy (self-government). Such autonomous churches maintain varying levels of dependence on their mother church, usually defined in the document of autonomy.
</p><p>Below is a list of the 6 autocephalous Orthodox churches forming the main body of Oriental Orthodox Christianity, all of which are titled equal to each other. Based on the definitions, the list is in the alphabetical order, with some of their constituent autonomous churches and exarchates listed as well.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mother_See_of_Holy_Etchmiadzin" title="Mother See of Holy Etchmiadzin">Mother See of Holy Etchmiadzin</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Holy_See_of_Cilicia" title="Holy See of Cilicia">Holy See of Cilicia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Armenian_Patriarchate_of_Constantinople" title="Armenian Patriarchate of Constantinople">Armenian Patriarchate of Constantinople</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Armenian_Patriarchate_of_Jerusalem" title="Armenian Patriarchate of Jerusalem">Armenian Patriarchate of Jerusalem</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/French_Coptic_Orthodox_Church" title="French Coptic Orthodox Church">French Coptic Orthodox Church</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eritrean_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Eritrean Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Eritrean Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Malankara_Orthodox_Syrian_Church" title="Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church">Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church_of_Antioch" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac Orthodox Church of Antioch">Syriac Orthodox Church of Antioch</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jacobite_Syrian_Christian_Church" title="Jacobite Syrian Christian Church">Jacobite Syrian Christian Church</a></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>There are a number of churches considered non-canonical, but whose members and clergy may or may not be in communion with the greater Oriental Orthodox communion. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Orthodox_Church" title="Celtic Orthodox Church">Celtic Orthodox Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_British_Church" title="Ancient British Church">Ancient British Church</a>, and lately the <a href="/wiki/British_Orthodox_Church" title="British Orthodox Church">British Orthodox Church</a>. These organizations have passed in and out of official recognition, but members rarely face excommunication when recognition is ended. The primates of these churches are typically referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Episcopi_vagantes" class="mw-redirect" title="Episcopi vagantes">episcopi vagantes</a></i> or <i>vagantes</i> in short.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_disputes">Internal disputes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Internal disputes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are numerous ongoing internal disputes within the Oriental Orthodox churches. These disputes result in lesser or greater degrees of impaired communion.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armenian_Apostolic">Armenian Apostolic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Armenian Apostolic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The least divisive of these disputes is within the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a>, between the Catholicosate of <a href="/wiki/Etchmiadzin_Cathedral" title="Etchmiadzin Cathedral">Etchmiadzin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See_of_Cilicia" title="Holy See of Cilicia">Catholicosate of the Great House of Cilicia</a>. The division of the two Catholicosates stemmed from frequent relocations of church headquarters due to political and military upheavals.
</p><p>The division between the two sees intensified during the Soviet period. By some Western bishops and clergy the Holy See of Etchmiadzin was seen as a captive Communist puppet. Sympathizers of this established congregations independent of Etchmiadzin, declaring loyalty instead to the See based in <a href="/wiki/Antelias" title="Antelias">Antelias</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>. The division was formalized in 1956 when the Antelias (Cilician) See broke away from the Etchmiadzin See. Though recognising the supremacy of the <a href="/wiki/Catholicos_of_All_Armenians" title="Catholicos of All Armenians">Catholicos of All Armenians</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Armenian_Catholicoi_of_Cilicia" title="List of Armenian Catholicoi of Cilicia">Catholicos of Cilicia</a> administers the clergy and dioceses independently. The dispute, however, has not at all caused a breach in communion between the two churches.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ethiopia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1992, following the abdication of <a href="/wiki/Abune_Merkorios" title="Abune Merkorios">Abune Merkorios</a> and election of <a href="/wiki/Abune_Paulos" title="Abune Paulos">Abune Paulos</a>, some Ethiopian Orthodox bishops in the United States maintained that the new election was invalid, and declared their independence from the Addis Ababa administration forming separate synod.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt1992-09-22_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1992-09-22-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> On 27 July 2018 representatives from both synods reached an agreement. According to the terms of the agreement, Abune Merkorios was reinstated as Patriarch alongside Abune Mathias (successor of Abune Paulos), who will continue to be responsible for administrative duties, and the two synods were merged into one synod, with any excommunications between them lifted.<sup id="cite_ref-DickinsonSchismMended_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DickinsonSchismMended-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Indians who follow the Oriental Orthodox faith belong to the <a href="/wiki/Malankara_Orthodox_Syrian_Church" title="Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church">Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_Syrian_Christian_Church" title="Jacobite Syrian Christian Church">Jacobite Syrian Christian Church</a>. The church split in two churches were united before 1912 and again from 1958 after reconciliation efforts but again separated in 1975. The <i>Malankara Orthodox</i> also known as Indian Orthodox Church is an autocephalous church. It is headed by the <a href="/wiki/Catholicos_of_the_East" title="Catholicos of the East">Catholicos of the East</a> and Malankara Metropolitan. The <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_Syrian_Christian_Church" title="Jacobite Syrian Christian Church">Jacobite Syrian Christian Church</a> is an autonomous body of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a> in India. It is headed by regional head <a href="/wiki/Catholicos_of_India" title="Catholicos of India">Catholicos of India</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Malabar_Independent_Syrian_Church" title="Malabar Independent Syrian Church">Malabar Independent Syrian Church</a> also follows the Oriental Orthodox tradition but is not in communion with other Oriental Orthodox churches.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occasional_confusions">Occasional confusions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Occasional confusions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a> is sometimes incorrectly described as an Oriental Orthodox church, though its origins lie in disputes that predated the Council of Chalcedon and it follows a different Christology from Oriental Orthodoxy. The historical <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a> was the church of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a> and declared itself separate from the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">state church of the Roman Empire</a> in 424–27, years before Chalcedon. Theologically, the Church of the East was affiliated with the doctrine of Nestorianism, and thus rejected the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Ephesus" title="Council of Ephesus">Council of Ephesus</a>, which declared <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a> heretical in 431. The Christology of the Oriental Orthodox churches in fact developed as a reaction against Nestorian Christology, which emphasizes the distinctness of the human and divine natures of Christ.
</p><p>There are many overlapping <a href="/wiki/Saint_Thomas_Christians" title="Saint Thomas Christians">ecclesiastical jurisdictions in India</a>, mostly with a Syriac liturgical heritage centered in the state of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>. The autonomous <a href="/wiki/Jacobite_Syrian_Christian_Church" title="Jacobite Syrian Christian Church">Jacobite Syrian Christian Church</a>, which comes under the Syriac Orthodox Church, is quite often confused with the autocephalous <a href="/wiki/Malankara_Orthodox_Syrian_Church" title="Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church">Malankara Orthodox Syrian Church</a> as the similarity in their names.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Oriental_Orthodox_Churches&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
